On one hand, there's the promise of modernisation and efficiency; on the other, a growing playground for cybercriminals. Drawing insights from IBM's latest Cost of a Data Breach Report, which spanned 16 countries, it's clear that the financial sector bears the brunt of a huge number of the world's cyber incidents, ranking second globally in terms of damage. Alarmingly, financial institutions face the steepest costs from cyberattacks. Losses average nearly USD $5.9 million per incident in the sector. This article aims to shed light on the cyber threats that loom over the financial sector, the impact of these attacks, and the most significant breaches for the sector over the past year. Table of Contents Key Takeaways The Biggest Cyber Threats to Financial Institutions The Financial Toll of Cyberattacks Security Operations Centre (SOC) Services for Financial Services The Biggest Cyber Breaches in the Financial Services Sector Key Takeaways The financial sector remains one of the most targeted industries globally, with the second-highest average cost per breach — $5.9 million — due to the sensitive nature of financial data and services. Ransomware is the leading threat, accounting for 63% of cyberattacks on financial institutions, with growing use of loaders, trojans, and data-wiping malware. Attack vectors are shifting, with a significant rise in the exploitation of software vulnerabilities and supply chain attacks, as social engineering tactics decline. Slow detection remains a critical risk, with breaches taking an average of 280 days to detect and contain — increasing both financial and reputational damage. A Security Operations Centre (SOC) is essential for financial services, offering 24/7 real-time threat detection, enhanced visibility, regulatory compliance (e.g. DORA), and immediate incident response. Breaches like the LockBit attack on BSI, ICBC's supply chain compromise, and MOVEit Transfer exploits highlight the urgent need for proactive cyber defence in finance. The Biggest Cyber Threats to Financial Institutions There's unfortunately a vast toolkit at the fingertips of malicious actors, but malware stands out as the prime adversary for financial institutions. Ransomware accounted for 63% of these cyberattacks in the past year, a huge increase from 18% the previous year. Financial institutions also grappled with other malware types such as loaders, remote control trojans, spyware, banking trojans, and data wiping malware. The last year has marked a pivotal shift in attack methodologies. While social engineering attacks dipped from 47% to 25%, the exploitation of software vulnerabilities saw a significant uptick. Supply chain attacks emerged as a significant concern for businesses, with cybercriminals leveraging weaknesses in organisations' supply chains across multiple industries to disseminate malware. The Financial Toll of Cyberattacks The economic impact of cyber incidents is staggering. According to IBM, the global average cost of a cyberbreach has climbed by 2.3%, reaching USD 4.45 million. Financial entities, however, bore a heavier burden, with losses hovering around USD $5.9 million per incident. Geographically, the US experienced the highest average data breach cost at USD $9.48 million. In contrast, the UK saw a 16% reduction in average costs, falling to USD $4.21 million and exiting the top five most impacted regions across the globe. The period of time it takes to identify and contain a breach – which averaged 207 and 73 days respectively according to the same IBM report – highlights a critical vulnerability in many organisations' cybersecurity defences. This prolonged detection and response time is not just a number; it represents a window of opportunity for cybercriminals to exploit compromised systems, extract sensitive data, and inflict substantial financial and reputational damage on institutions. Visibility into an organisation's digital infrastructure is the key to reducing the timeframe of undetected cyber activities Without comprehensive monitoring and the ability to detect anomalies in real-time, breaches can remain unnoticed for extended periods, exacerbating the impact and cost of the incident. Herein lies the indispensable value of a Security Operations Centre (SOC) service. Security Operations Centre (SOC) Services for Financial Services A Security Operations Centre (SOC) acts as the nerve centre for cybersecurity, providing 24/7 monitoring of an organisation's entire infrastructure, networks, systems, and data. A SOC employs advanced technologies, including artificial intelligence (AI) and machine learning (ML), alongside a team of cybersecurity experts to continuously monitor and analyse activity and security posture. This constant vigilance ensures that potential threats are identified swiftly, allowing for immediate action to contain and mitigate the effects of any breach. A SOC service enhances an organisation's cybersecurity framework by offering: Real-Time Detection and Response: Immediate identification of suspicious activity minimises the window for attackers to operate, significantly reducing potential damage. Full Visibility: By integrating data from across the digital landscape, a SOC provides a holistic view of a company's threat environment, enabling more effective detection and analysis of complex threats. Expertise and Advanced Technologies: SOC teams are equipped with the expertise and cutting-edge technologies necessary to tackle the evolving threats in financial institutions' cyber landscape, ensuring that defences remain robust against sophisticated attacks. Regulatory Compliance: With increasing regulatory demands regarding data protection and breach reporting, including DORA, a SOC helps ensure compliance by implementing industry-standard security measures and providing detailed incident reports. The integration of a SOC into an organisation's cybersecurity strategy is not just an enhancement; it's a fundamental component that shifts the focus from reactive to proactive defence. By significantly shortening the time to detect and respond to threats, a SOC service is instrumental in minimising the impact of cyberattacks, safeguarding the integrity of financial institutions, and maintaining the trust of their customers. The Biggest Cyber Breaches in the Financial Services Sector Several high-profile incidents underscore the severity of the threat landscape for Financial Services over the last year: LockBit Ransomware Attack on BSI: LockBit targeted BSI, one of Indonesia's largest banks, leaking 1.5 TB of confidential data after the bank refused to pay the USD $20 million ransom. ICBC Ransomware Attack: Vulnerabilities in the supply chain of the US arm of ICBC opened the door to a ransomware attack, that disrupted US Treasuries trading. It's reported that the ransom was paid to restore access and get business operations live in this scenario. Law enforcement does not encourage, endorse, nor condone the payment of ransom demands and it is advised that companies don't pay ransoms by the NCSC. MOVEit Application Vulnerabilities: A zero-day vulnerability discovered in MOVEit Transfer – a secure data transfer application – led to multiple data breaches across several Financial Services organisations, prompting a crucial patch update to be released in June. Open-source Software Attacks: A series of targeted attacks on the finance sector came via open-source software, Checkmarx. These attacks highlighted the need for stronger defences to be in place and industry-wide collaboration to further bolster defences. The financial sector's cybersecurity landscape is complex and ever evolving. Financial institutions must remain vigilant and proactive in their defense strategies to mitigate the risks of cyberattacks. Guide Contact 925-289-9962 Cybersecurity Statistics 2025: Comprehensive Data on Global Threats, Breaches, and Attack Trends December 2, 2025 Anish Devasia The global cybersecurity statistics for 2025 reveal an alarming escalation in cyber threats that demand immediate attention from business leaders. Organizations face unprecedented cyber risks as cybercrime costs are projected to reach $10.5 trillion annually, and data breach expenses average $4.44 million globally. Ransomware attacks have surged to compromise 44% of all breaches, while AI-powered phishing attacks have exploded by over 4,000% since late 2022. Understanding these cybersecurity statistics is the first step toward building effective defenses against an increasingly sophisticated, evolving threat landscape. Quick Summary The 2025 cybersecurity landscape presents critical challenges every business must understand: Cybercrime will cost $10.5 trillion globally in 2025, making it the third-largest economy after the US and China. Average data breach costs reached $4.44 million globally, with US organizations facing $10.22 million per incident. Ransomware is involved in 44% of all data breaches, with median ransom demands reaching $1 million. Phishing attacks increased by 4,151% since ChatGPT's release, with AI-generated content in 82.6% of phishing emails. Supply chain attacks doubled beginning in April 2025, with third-party breaches comprising 35.5% of all incidents. Organizations take an average of 204 days to detect breaches, giving attackers months of undetected access. What Are the Key Cybersecurity Statistics to Know Global Breach Costs: Regional Disparities Emerge
The cybersecurity threats of 2025 reflect fundamental shifts in both attacker capabilities and organizational vulnerabilities. The global average cost of a breach, averaging $4.44 million, represents a 9% decline from 2024's $4.88 million, yet this statistic masks critical regional variations. Key Finding: United States organizations experienced record-breaking average breach costs of $10.22 million—the highest ever documented for any region. Industry-Specific Breach Costs Healthcare remains the most expensive sector for security breaches at $7.42 million per incident, followed by the financial sector at $5.9 million. These elevated costs reflect stringent regulatory requirements, operational disruption concerns, and the sensitive data these industries manage. The Human Element in Cyber Breaches Critical Stat: Social engineering tactics are used in 68% of breaches, with phishing accounting for 16% of incidents that cost an average of $4.88 million each. What This Means: Organizations implementing comprehensive security measures and awareness training reduce phishing incident rates by 86% within twelve months, demonstrating the measurable ROI of human element-focused security programs. Detection Timeline: A Critical Vulnerability Surprising Finding: The average breach goes undetected for 204 days, giving threat actors nearly 7 months to exfiltrate data, escalate privileges, and establish persistence mechanisms. Organizations deploying AI-powered security tools cut this timeline to approximately 102 days—still measured in months rather than the hours or days required for effective incident response. How Is the Global Cyber Security Landscape Changing? Cybercrime as a Global Economic Force The global cybersecurity environment has transformed from isolated technical incidents into a systemic economic threat affecting national security and business operations worldwide. Stat Box: Cybercrime's projected $10.5 trillion annual cost positions it as the third-largest global economy, surpassing the GDP of most nations. AI-Powered Attack Evolution Attackers increasingly leverage artificial intelligence throughout their operations. A research study analyzing 2,800 ransomware incidents found that 80% incorporated AI capabilities for malware development, phishing content generation, and social engineering. Speed Advantage: Generative AI enables threat actors to create convincing phishing emails in approximately five minutes—a task previously requiring 16 hours of human effort. Nation-State Threat Escalation Nation-state actors have escalated their targeting of commercial infrastructure. Campaigns like Salt Typhoon successfully infiltrated major telecommunications networks for espionage, while Volt Typhoon's prepositioning operations raised concerns about potential disruption capabilities within critical infrastructure. Emerging Threat: Chinese state-sponsored groups increased their targeting of software supply chains and managed service providers by 200-300% compared to the previous year. The Widening Skills Gap The cybersecurity professionals' skills gap expanded by 8% since 2024, with two-thirds of organizations reporting moderate-to-critical skills shortages. Key Takeaway: Only 14% of organizations express confidence in having adequate personnel and expertise to meet their security requirements, creating fundamental organizational vulnerabilities that persist despite investments in technical security services. What Do the Latest Data Breach and Cyber Attack Statistics Reveal? Vulnerability Exploitation at Scale
Recent data breach statistics demonstrate both the scale and sophistication of contemporary cyberattacks. The first half of 2025 witnessed 23,667 CVE (Common Vulnerabilities and Exposures) publications, representing a 16% increase over the same period in 2024. Critical Numbers: Threat actors actively exploited 161 vulnerabilities 42% had public proof-of-concept exploits available 69% of exploited vulnerabilities required no authentication for successful compromise Nearly one-third enabled remote code execution capabilities Microsoft products accounted for 17% of all exploitations Record-Breaking Credential Breach Major Incident Alert: The June 2025 breach, exposing 16 billion login credentials across 30+ datasets, represents one of the largest credential compromises ever recorded. The leaked data included: Usernames and passwords Session tokens and cookies Metadata linked to major platforms (Facebook, Google, Apple, GitHub, Telegram) This cyber incident, likely originating from infostealer malware campaigns, highlighted both the massive scale of stolen credentials and the inadequacy of many information security practices. The Patching Problem Surprising Finding: Vulnerability exploitation increased by 34% year-over-year, with attackers leveraging automated scanning and exploitation tools to compromise unpatched systems at scale. Despite this threat, almost half of the identified vulnerabilities remain unresolved even when patches are publicly available, reflecting organizational challenges in testing, deploying, and managing security updates. How Common Are Ransomware Attacks Ransomware Dominates the Threat Landscape Ransomware attacks have become the dominant cybersecurity threat, accounting for 44% of all data breaches and representing a 12% year-over-year increase. Industry Impact: The manufacturing sector experienced an 87% spike in ransomware attacks, making it the top target for four consecutive years, driven by operational dependencies and legacy technology vulnerabilities. Ransomware-as-a-Service Proliferation Ransomware-as-a-Service (RaaS) platforms have commoditized sophisticated attacks, enabling actors with limited technical skills to conduct professional-grade campaigns. Growth Statistics: 96 active ransomware groups operated by mid-2025 41% increase from 68 groups in the first half of 2024 Median ransom demands: $1 million 63% of demands exceed $1 million 30% surpass $5 million The Double Extortion Model Current Standard: Double extortion has become the standard operating model, with 96% of ransomware cases involving both encryption and data exfiltration. Rather than relying solely on encryption, threat actors steal sensitive data before encrypting systems, threatening public release to maximize pressure. Some operations have evolved into triple and quadruple extortion, adding: DDoS attacks Targeting individual data subjects Coordinating social engineering campaigns Financial Impact: Beyond the Ransom Key Takeaway: Recovery costs consistently exceed ransom payments, averaging $2.73 million, compared to ransom payments averaging $2 million. SMB Targeting: 70% of ransomware incidents hit small and medium-sized businesses 88% of small business breaches involve ransomware 63% of organizations reported declining to pay ransoms in 2025 (up from 59% in 2024) How Big a Threat Are Phishing and Social Engineering Attacks? Social Engineering as the Top Attack Vector
Phishing and social engineering attacks represent the most effective attack vectors for gaining initial network security access, with the human element involved in 68% of all breaches. Stat Box: Social engineering accounted for 36% of all incident response cases between May 2024 and May 2025, making it the top initial access method. AI Transforms Phishing Effectiveness The integration of artificial intelligence has transformed phishing from a labor-intensive discipline into an automated, high-volume attack vector. Explosive Growth: Phishing attacks increased by 4,151% since ChatGPT's November 2022 release. By 2025, AI-generated content appeared in 82.6% of phishing emails, with threat actors leveraging language models to craft perfect prose, incorporate personalized details, and create contextually appropriate narratives that bypass traditional security filters. Industry-Specific Vulnerability Rankings Most Susceptible Industries: Healthcare and pharmaceuticals: 41.9% Insurance: 39.2% Retail and wholesale: 36.5% Training Impact: Organizations implementing comprehensive security awareness training demonstrate measurable improvements, reducing phishing attacks by 86% within 12 months and achieving a 3.2% failure rate in phishing simulations. Emerging AI-Powered Threats Vishing (Voice Phishing): Attacks surged 442% in the second half of 2024, leveraging AI-generated voice clones and synthetic scripts. Deepfake Financial Fraud: Deepfake technology enables the creation of fake images, audio, and video communications. A Hong Kong company lost $25 million when employees received a video call from someone who appeared to be their CFO, requesting a wire transfer—only to discover it was a sophisticated deepfake. Business Email Compromise: BEC attacks increased 13% in the first three months of 2025. One notable case involved fraudulent invoices sent to Google and Facebook that impersonated a legitimate hardware supplier, resulting in combined losses of over $100 million. What Do Malware and Virus Attack Statistics Show? Vulnerability Exploitation Patterns The malware landscape continues to evolve, driven by the integration of artificial intelligence and new delivery mechanisms. First Half 2025 Exploitation Statistics: Attackers exploited 161 distinct vulnerabilities 69% required no authentication 30% enabled remote code execution AI-Powered Malware Evolution Machine learning now enables malware to: Mutate dynamically to evade detection. Identify and avoid sandbox environments. Adapt behavior based on deployed endpoint defenses. What This Means: This transformation renders traditional signature-based detection approaches increasingly ineffective, requiring organizations to implement advanced behavioral analytics and anomaly detection through integrated security platforms. Mobile Malware Acceleration
2025 Mobile Threat Statistics: 11 new mobile malware strains discovered 9 additional strains resurged during the first half of 2025 Android banking trojans adopted virtualization-based overlays and NFC relay attacks Notable Threat: SuperCard X, a Chinese Malware-as-a-Service platform, emerged, enabling NFC relay fraud by capturing and transmitting contactless card data to attacker-controlled devices. IoT Device Targeting Alarming Stat: Internet-connected devices face approximately 820,000 hacking attempts daily, a 46% year-over-year surge. Scale of the Problem: 35.2 billion connected devices projected globally in 2025 Kaspersky blocked over 629 million cyber attacks from online resources in Q1 2025 alone The average home network faces 10 attack attempts every 24 hours Rising Cost of Cybercrime and Business Impact The Economic Burden of Cybercrime The economic impact of cybercrime extends beyond direct breach costs into profound business disruption, regulatory penalties, and long-term reputational damage. Economic Scale: Global cybercrime costs reached $10.5 trillion annually in 2025 and are projected to climb to $12.2 trillion by 2031, an extraordinary burden exceeding most national GDPs. Breach Cost Breakdown Average breach costs of $4.44 million globally include: Incident response Forensic investigation Notification expenses Regulatory fines Business interruption Customer data protection and restoration Regional Disparity: US organizations face costs of $10.22 million per breach—more than double the global average. The Time-Cost Relationship Critical Finding: Breach lifecycle duration significantly impacts the cost of a data breach. Breaches detected and contained within 200 days: $3.87 million Breaches exceeding 200 days: $5.01 million Cost premium for delayed detection: 29% Defensive Investment ROI: Organizations implementing zero-trust architectures and AI-powered security operations reduced breach costs by an average of $1.76 million compared to organizations maintaining traditional perimeter-based security. Supply Chain Breach Economics Supply chain attacks involving third-party vendors averaged $4.91 million in costs and took longer to identify and contain than directly targeted attacks. Accelerating Trend: Third-party involvement was present in 35.5% of all breaches— double the previous year's rate—reflecting attackers' recognition that compromising a single vendor can provide legitimate access to potentially hundreds or thousands of downstream customers. Small Business Existential Threat SMB Impact Statistics: 78% express fear that a major incident could force them out of business SMBs experience targeting nearly 4 times more frequently than large organizations 94% experienced at least one cyber attack in 2024 What Emerging Technologies Are Shaping Future Cyber Threats?
Artificial Intelligence: Double-Edged Sword Artificial intelligence represents both the most transformative defensive technology and the most rapidly escalating attack vector among top cyber threats. 2025 AI Threat Statistics: 16% of reported cyber incidents involved attackers leveraging AI tools for sophisticated social engineering AI capabilities now power 80% of ransomware campaigns. Tasks traditionally requiring weeks can now be completed in minutes Generative AI creates effective phishing emails in approximately 5 minutes vs. 16 hours of human error-prone manual effort. AI Agents: The Next Evolution Emerging Threat: The emergence of AI agents represents an even more sophisticated evolution, though such agents haven't yet been detected in meaningful numbers in active attacks. Cybersecurity leaders worry that AI agents capable of designing workflows and using tools to achieve complex objectives might enable attackers to automate substantially more than phishing variations, potentially: Collecting information on targets Analyzing data patterns Formulating attack plans Generating customized social engineering content at scale Quantum Computing: Harvest Now, Decrypt Later Strategic Threat: Quantum computing has transitioned from a theoretical concern to a practical planning requirement. While cryptographically relevant quantum computers don't yet exist at scale, sophisticated adversaries actively employ “harvest now, decrypt later” strategies—capturing and storing currently encrypted critical data for future decryption when quantum capabilities mature. Preparation Timeline: NIST selected four post-quantum encryption algorithms designed to withstand quantum computer attacks, though transitioning to these standards will require 10-20 years from standardization to full integration. IoT Botnet Infrastructure Record-Breaking Attack: The Aisuru botnet demonstrated unprecedented scale, reaching 29.6 terabits per second in outbound traffic on October 6, 2025—one of the largest DDoS attacks ever recorded. Botnet Composition: Compromised approximately 300,000 hosts worldwide Primarily, consumer-grade routers, security cameras, and DVRs Exploited insecure firmware or default credentials DDoS Attack Proliferation 2025 DDoS Statistics: Exceeded 8 million incidents in the first half of 2025 Sustained intensities reaching 3.12 terabits per second DDoS-as-a-Service platforms enable anyone to rent powerful botnets for $20-40 monthly Which Industries Face the Highest Cybersecurity Risks Healthcare: Highest Breach Costs The healthcare industry faces the most severe cybersecurity risks, with average breach costs of $7.42 million and ransomware attacks growing by at least 25%. Attack Frequency: Healthcare organizations represent the third-most attacked industry worldwide 68% of officials report an average of two attacks annually Operational disruption creates immediate pressure to restore operations regardless of cost Financial Services: High-Value Targets Financial and insurance companies face the second-highest breach costs, averaging ** $5.9 million**, up 2.3% year over year. Attack Vector Statistics: API and web application attacks increased 65% over the prior year Malicious bot requests spiked 69% Successful attacks directly translate to financial gain through fraudulent transactions and account takeovers. Manufacturing: Ransomware Magnet Persistent Targeting: Manufacturing has remained the top ransomware attack target for four consecutive years, with attacks spiking 87% year-over-year in 2024. Vulnerability Factors: Legacy equipment lacking modern security tools Slow patch application in production environments Real-time operational requirements prevent extended downtime Operational Technology Attacks Critical Infrastructure Threat: Transportation and manufacturing represent the top two most targeted sectors according to operational technology telemetry analysis. Key Stat: Approximately 75% of operational technology attacks start as information security breaches, with attackers exploiting compromised IT systems as pivot points for lateral movement into critical operational networks. Small and Medium-Sized Business Targeting Disproportionate Risk: SMBs are targeted nearly 4 times more frequently than large organizations 94% of SMBs experienced at least one cyber attack in 2024 Limited cybersecurity budgets and smaller security teams create efficient targets What Are the Best Strategies for Managing Cyber Risks and Building Cyber Resilience
Zero-Trust Architecture Zero-Trust Architecture has emerged as the preeminent framework for addressing the persistent sophistication of attacks and building cyber resilience. Core Principles: Continuous verification of all users, devices, and services Assumes breach risk is ever-present Enforces least-privilege access management Implements microsegmentation, preventing lateral movement Maintains continuous monitoring Financial Impact: Implementing a mature zero-trust framework can lower data breach costs by an average of $1.76 million. Multi-Factor Authentication Adoption Market Growth: Multi-factor authentication has become foundational to identity security, with the MFA market projected to reach $17.76 billion by 2025 and double to $40 billion by 2030. Adoption Gap: 87% among technology firms Only 27% of small businesses with fewer than 25 employees Regulatory mandates increasingly drive adoption, with federal agencies, financial institutions, and payment card data protection all facing MFA requirements. AI-Powered Security Operations AI-powered security services centers enable detection and response at machine speed, with AI-driven analysis: Reducing alert volume Identifying at-risk data Spotting security gaps Enabling faster incident response Cost Savings: Organizations that extensively use security AI and automation realize annual average cost savings of $2.22 million compared to those without such capabilities. Continuous Vulnerability Management Continuous vulnerability management has become essential, replacing periodic approaches, with organizations needing to identify, prioritize, remediate, and verify patching in continuous cycles through risk management processes. Challenge: Only a fraction of perimeter-device vulnerabilities are fully remediated despite public patch availability, reflecting organizational challenges in testing patches, managing compatibility, and scheduling deployment windows. Incident Response Preparedness Preparedness Gap: Incident response preparedness remains inadequate across most organizations, with only 30% regularly testing incident response plans despite clear documentation of cost savings. Financial Impact: Organizations with fully documented and regularly tested response protocols pay approximately 58% less per breach than those without structured response procedures. Security Awareness Training Effectiveness Proven ROI: Security awareness training demonstrates measurable effectiveness, reducing phishing attacks by 86% within 12 months through behavior-based programs that incorporate simulations with feedback, addressing human error as a primary vulnerability. Network Segmentation and Architecture Proper network security segmentation forms the foundation of effective cybersecurity posture, preventing lateral movement when attackers gain initial access. Security begins with a properly designed, segmented network architecture that: Isolates critical systems Implements access management controls Enables monitoring of all traffic flows Third-Party Risk Management: Organizations must: Assess the cybersecurity risks of third-party service providers Maintain the software supply chain's documentation Conduct regular vendor security assessments Implement incident response playbooks addressing third-party compromise scenarios Frequently Asked Questions Is Cybersecurity Still in High Demand? Demand for cybersecurity professionals continues to accelerate far beyond workforce supply, creating significant cybersecurity job opportunities. Employment Projections: • Based on Bureau of Labor Statistics data, information security analyst employment is anticipated to expand by 35% between 2021 and 2031, representing a rate well above the national average for job growth. • CyberSeek documents 663,434 unfilled cybersecurity positions against 1,129,659 employed cybersecurity professionals • The cyber skills gap has increased 8% since 2024 Only 14% of organizations reported confidence in having adequate personnel and expertise. Specialization Shortage: This shortage extends beyond traditional security professionals' roles into specialized areas, including: • Cloud security • Threat intelligence analysis • Malware reverse engineering • Incident response Recruiting and retaining skilled professionals remains difficult, with rising demand and niche skill requirements driving competition across all industries. What Is the #1 Cybersecurity Threat Today? Ransomware is the dominant cybersecurity threat in 2025, accounting for 44% of all data breaches and experiencing a 12% year-over-year increase. Evolution Beyond Encryption: The threat landscape has evolved beyond traditional encryption-focused attacks into sophisticated multi-extortion operations combining: • Encryption against data theft • DDoS attacks • Threats to expose sensitive data publicly Scale of Operations: • Ransomware-as-a-Service platforms have commoditized sophisticated attacks • 96 active groups operating by mid-2025 • The manufacturing sector experienced a 87% spike in attacks • 70% of ransomware incidents hit SMBs Financial Impact: Average recovery costs reached $2.73 million, consistently exceeding average ransom payments of $2 million. The operational disruption and financial impact extend far beyond initial infection into prolonged business disruption and reputational damage. How Many Cyberattacks Happen Daily? IoT Targeting Alone: Internet-connected devices alone experience approximately 820,000 hacking attempts per day, a 46% year-over-year increase. Broader Attack Statistics: • Q1 2025: Kaspersky blocked over 629 million cyber attacks from online resources • An average home network faces approximately 10 attack attempts every 24 hours • Enterprise networks experience substantially higher volumes • Distributed Denial of Service attacks exceeded 8 million incidents in the first half of 2025 alone Attack Nature: These statistics primarily reflect automated scanning and exploitation attempts rather than targeted, human-driven efforts, demonstrating the massive global infrastructure deployed to identify and exploit vulnerabilities at scale. The Bottom Line: Strategic Imperatives for 2025 The 2025 cybersecurity landscape demands immediate strategic action from security leaders who recognize that cyber threats represent existential cyber risks rather than mere technical challenges. The Threat Reality By the Numbers: Cybercrime costs: $10.5 trillion annually Average breach costs: $4.44 million globally Ransomware in 44% of breaches AI-powered attacks are accelerating at unprecedented rates Organizations cannot afford reactive security measures. Building Effective Defense Effective cybersecurity begins with foundational network infrastructure designed with security trends and principles embedded from the start: Proper network security segmentation Zero-trust architecture Multi-factor authentication AI-powered security operations Comprehensive incident response capabilities These defensive layers are necessary to detect, contain, and recover from inevitable attacks while building a minimum viable level of cyber resilience. Proven Results Organizations with properly segmented networks, robust access management controls, and continuous monitoring capabilities significantly reduce both the likelihood and the impact of successful cyberattacks. Measurable Outcomes: Organizations implementing zero-trust principles and AI-powered security operations reduce breach costs by millions of dollars. Detection and containment occur in half the time of their peers Properly tested incident response plans reduce breach costs by 58% The Investment Question As these evolving threats continue evolving, the question isn't whether to invest in comprehensive security policies and innovative solutions—it's whether organizations can afford not to. Cyber insurance can provide additional protection, but implementing robust cybersecurity efforts and proper data protection measures remains essential for long-term cyber resilience. Source: Driving NDAA Section 1505 Compliance for DOD with Nozomi Networks
URL: https://cdn.prod.website-files.com/645a4534705010e2cb244f50/648c8e5ccfee25748915c5af_Nozomi-Networks-NDAA-Section-1505-Compliance-White-Paper.pdf
WHITE PAPER Driving NDAA Section 1505 Compliance for DOD with Nozomi Networks
Section 1505 of the FY 2022 National Defense Authorization Act (NDAA) is a significant mandate from Congress to the Department of Defense (DOD) to protect its critical infrastructure and operational technology (CI/OT). It will require ongoing briefings in the next several years to Congress on the status of cybersecurity readiness and shed light on how it protects mission critical assets and operational infrastructure. Nozomi Networks can fulfill the complete Section 1505 mandate for asset visibility, vulnerability management and threat detection as a standalone solution. The deadline for initial reporting on asset visibility is coming up in November 2023 and Nozomi Networks can help towards that goal today. The attacks against critical infrastructure across industries have accelerated in recent years and pose potentially catastrophic risk to the nation’s infrastructure as well as military readiness. Backed by nation-state actors and well-funded non-governmental organizations, hackers have launched attacks against water treatment facilities, transportation systems, energy grids, pipelines, hospitals and more. The geopolitical motivation and financial objectives have never been greater. Our military operations have the same vulnerable systems and applications, including controllers, sensors, power generation, HVAC systems, life and safety systems and other computer- controlled infrastructure that monitor and drive critical processes. As in the private sector, the DOD has relied on operational engineers and technicians with few cybersecurity tools or little training to manage tens to hundreds of thousands of systems and devices. Security efforts within traditional IT has been the focus for over two decades with no programmatic policies on protecting the corresponding CI/OT environments. Now, DOD is required through the Section 1505 mandate to ensure and report on these critical infrastructure systems and their cybersecurity readiness as a matter of urgency with limited visibility and preparation.
Section 1505 Background Other critical cybersecurity guidelines need to be addressed as well. For example, the More Situational Awareness in Control Systems (MOSAICS) technology demonstration, developed jointly at Sandia National Lab, Idaho National Lab and John Hopkins University Applied Physics Lab, is a recommended design for the protection of control systems from cyberattack. MOSAICS is in fact referenced in Section 1505 and is an excellent model for securing CI/OT. MOSAICS is the first-ever comprehensive, integrated and automated cyber defense capability for industrial control systems. It demonstrates how system operators can quickly, easily and effectively detect and characterize cyberattacks against critical infrastructure in real time and will eventually provide support for automated response actions. MOSAICS is a broader set of integrated security capabilities than Section 1505 requires, however. Additionally, in early March, 2023, the Biden-Harris administration released the National Cybersecurity Strategy that shifts the security burden from individuals to larger, national defense organizations that are better equipped to address the growing threats from state-sponsored actors to critical infrastructure. The strategy prioritizes defending critical infrastructure, “giving the American people confidence in the availability and resilience of our critical infrastructure and the essential services it provides.” This White House cybersecurity strategy is another urgent call to action to deploy baseline visualization and monitoring capabilities for critical infrastructure in response to well- documented attacks against physical control systems. The strategy acknowledges that OT defenses are not as operationally mature as IT cyber Techniques, Tactics and Procedures (TTP), and that Command-and-Control IT infrastructure can be degraded by attacks on the OT infrastructure they rely on, e.g., power and communication systems.
MOSAICS Program and New Policy Requirements Prioritize Control System Cybersecurity
The Nozomi Platform is a unified asset visibility, vulnerability management and threat detection solution for operational networks and devices. It is built on an Artificial Intelligence/ Machine Learning (AI/ML) engine that provides deeper insight, data analytics and situational awareness than traditional solutions. The platform is uniquely designed for critical infrastructure and operational networks with an emphasis on low-impact, non-disruptive passive network monitoring, policy-based asset discovery and threat detection, with continuous endpoint monitoring and vulnerability management. Nozomi Networks provides asset aware deep packet analysis over the broadest control system network protocols to detect anomalies and potential threats with greater accuracy. 3. The Nozomi Networks Platform as a Solution for NDAA Section 1505 Reporting The Nozomi Platform provides a complete asset inventory and awareness of existing network connections, traffic patterns and anomalies. Nozomi Networks manages one of the most extensive databases of known vulnerabilities from researchers and security bodies around the globe to keep organizations up-to- date with threat signatures and indicators of compromise (IOC). Endpoint sensors can be applied to critical hosts to provide continuous visibility to endpoint asset configuration, operator activity, and ever-changing vulnerability/threat posture across the critical process attack surface. Going beyond cybersecurity issues, the Nozomi Platform monitors process variables in operational equipment, learning baseline behavior to detect anomalies that can indicate potential issues or predict maintenance requirements. Our learned behavior over time helps eliminate false alerts and provides deeper insight into trends for critical systems and processes.
When it’s time to respond to a security breach or a process control issue, you need actionable intelligence to address the problem with the minimum cost and impact to your operations. Nozomi Networks gives you all the information and insight right at your fingertips to remediate issues, dive into further research, coordinate and prioritize activities and minimize time to response. In general, the Nozomi Platform can assist DOD in meeting NDAA Section 1505 requirements by providing complete visibility for operational control systems to interconnections, dependencies, component risks and threats/risks to process functionality. Nozomi Networks draws from one of the world’s largest repositories of threat details to quickly identify threats and provides details to assist remediation efforts. 3. The Nozomi Networks Platform as a Solution for NDAA Section 1505 Reporting
Specific Section 1505 Mandates Mapping to Nozomi Platform Capabilities Directs the DOD to complete a mapping of mission-relevant terrain in cyberspace for Defense Critical Assets and Task Critical Assets at sufficient granularity to enable mission thread analysis and situational awareness, including identification of access vectors; network topologies; reliant weapon systems; and cybersecurity defenses across information and operational technology. Nozomi Networks can provide a complete asset inventory, vulnerability analysis and communications map for all operational assets and devices automatically and keep it up to date. Coupled with continuous monitoring of network traffic and threats, Nozomi Networks ensures complete visibility and situational awareness for the relevant terrain in cyberspace. With the initial operational readiness reporting dates coming up in November 2023, Nozomi Networks can provide the required initial asset visibility and vulnerability assessments today. Below, we cover how the Nozomi Platform can help meet specific mandates in Section 1505, Operational Technology and Mission-relevant Terrain in Cyberspace: Directs the Combatant Commands to develop, institute, and modify their internal processes, responsibilities, and functions to enable effective mission thread analysis, cyber situational awareness, and effective cyber defense of Defense Critical Assets and Task Critical Assets. The Nozomi Platform can help prioritize risks and which vulnerabilities need to be addressed to have the most impact. Our cyber defense playbook feature allows organizations to document and track remediation procedures to improve efficiency. Directs the DOD Chief Information Officer (CIO) to establish or change policies, control systems standards, risk management framework and authority-to-operate policies, and cybersecurity reference architectures to provide baseline cybersecurity requirements for operational technology across the Department of Defense Information Network (DODIN). The Nozomi Platform is a key component of any risk management framework with its ability to verify operational networks against all known vulnerabilities, and perform rapid risk assessments.
With deep understanding of operational networks and devices and continuous vigilance over the entire domain, Nozomi Networks keeps organizations on top of identified vulnerabilities and can prioritize risk reduction efforts to maximize efficiency. Directs the USCYBERCOM to update the mission, scope, and posture of Joint Forces Headquarters- Department of Defense Information Network (JFHQ-DODIN) to ensure appropriate visibility of operational technology and weapon systems and that United States Cyber Command (USCYBERCOM) can effectively defend such operational technology. This includes having established processes for incident and compliance reporting; ensuring compliance with DOD cybersecurity policy; and ensuring that cyber vulnerabilities, attack vectors, and security violations are appropriately managed. Key features in the Nozomi Platform allow for the definition, customization, and tracking of established incident response procedures. Existing procedures can easily be imported into the Nozomi Platform. With a primary focus on visibility to operational technology and the ability to monitor potential threats, we can provide the strongest assurances towards operational readiness to defend such infrastructure. 4. Specific Section 1505 Mandates Mapping to Nozomi Platform Capabilities
Let’s look at how Nozomi Networks is deployed in other operational environments such as a shipping and port logistics infrastructure to see how the same capabilities can be applied to DOD operations. A maritime transportation system, including port logistics, has a complicated and diverse set of operational security needs and devices. These are usually highly integrated systems that extend from shipboard operations to cargo management, physical security infrastructure and communications. While access controls to the networks and applications are highly protected, many of the individual sensors, cameras, crane operations or safety systems represent potential areas of vulnerability, access or compromise. Nozomi Networks provides visibility, asset monitoring, vulnerability management and threat detection for this diverse environment. The amount of security event data and asset inventory details could be overwhelming to many operational security teams, but the Nozomi Platform helps automate many of the tedious analytical tasks to quickly prioritize which alerts and anomalies require the most immediate attention. With many unique connectivity, bandwidth and protocol requirements, Nozomi Networks is ideally suited to integrate and monitor these types of OT environments where traditional IT security and monitoring solutions fail. 5. Nozomi Networks in Other Critical Infrastructure Environments A shipping transportation and port operations system is an excellent model for the complex terrain found on military bases and in military operations. The diversity of networked devices and potential vulnerabilities can pose a challenge for security platforms not designed for such OT challenges. Increasingly, maritime applications, like other critical infrastructure sectors, are coming under Federal guidelines and mandates to shore up cybersecurity defenses and defining requirements to better audit and report on operational readiness. The NDAA mandates for reporting to Congress are fast approaching in November 2023. Organizations that are not fully prepared will need to immediately start on an asset visibility and vulnerability awareness project as the first phase of operational readiness. This is exactly where the Nozomi Networks platform aligns with DOD’s immediate reporting needs. Nozomi Networks provides a rapid deployable sensing capability for detailed mapping of not only the OT networks and components but to baseline the processes that are critical to fully understanding system interdependencies and vulnerabilities. Developed as a core Intrusion Detection System (IDS) for critical control systems, in defense sensor platforms, the Nozomi Platform can be considered an Intelligence, Surveillance, and Reconnaissance (ISR) system for OT/IOT.
Nozomi Networks combines a critical control system sensing system that provides ISR (CS-ISR) with threat detection and asset visibility, advanced vulnerability management, continuous monitoring and reconnaissance. We provide the Section 1505 requirements mapping to features such as asset identification and vulnerability tracking, risk management, threat detection and reporting services required by Congress. Let us show you how you can get started today. 6. Conclusion Nozomi Networks accelerates digital transformation by protecting the world’s critical infrastructure, industrial and government organizations from cyber threats. Our solution delivers exceptional network and asset visibility, threat detection, and insights for OT and IoT environments. Source: ETM Release 1.6

ETM Release 1.6 Skip To Main Content Enterprise TruRisk Management Release Notes Contents Enterprise TruRisk Management Release 1.8.1 Release 1.8 Release 1.7 Release 1.6.1 Release 1.6 Release 1.5.1 Release 1.5 Release 1.4 Release 1.3.2 Release 1.3.2 API Release 1.3.1 Release 1.3.1 API Release 1.3.0 Release 1.3.0 API Release 1.2.2 Release 1.2.1 Release 1.2.0 Release 1.2.0 API Connector Centralization for ETM Expand All Favorites × menu-close ☰ menu Home: Enterprise TruRisk Management ETM Release 1.6 Enterprise TruRisk Management Release 1.6 January 02, 2026 Introduced Unified Asset Inventory (UAI) The Unified Asset Inventory (UAI) provides a single, comprehensive view of all assets across your organization's hybrid IT environment. It consolidates asset data from Identity, Applications, Compute, Containers, Storage, Software, Network, Resources, and Certificates into a single inventory. UAI unifies asset discovery, classification, and context across different company modules and external integrations. This approach eliminates isolated views. As a result, every asset is visible, whether it is cloud-based, on-premises, or externally integrated. Additionally, all assets can be searched easily and assessed consistently. Prerequisite This feature is available on request; contact Qualys support or your Technical Account Manager (TAM) to activate it for your account. What's New with the Unified Asset Inventory Introduced a centralized asset inventory serving as the single source of truth for all asset types. Unified asset visibility across company-managed and externally discovered or integrated assets. Enabled cross-asset tagging to associate business services with infrastructure, applications, and identities. Added global search across all asset classes using hostnames, tags, domains, and service names. Laid the foundation for asset relationship mapping to support dependency and impact analysis. Unified Asset Tagging You can now apply the same tag across multiple asset types, enabling a business-centric view of your infrastructure. Example: Apply the tag Banking Service to on-prem hosts, cloud storage (S3), containers, web applications, and certificates. A single dashboard or report can then show all assets supporting the Banking Service along with their associated security findings. Unified Search Across All Asset Types UAI enables a single search experience across the entire asset landscape. You can search using: Hostnames or IPs Tag names (business services, environments, owners) Domains or application names Service or resource identifiers Search results return all related assets across compute, containers, storage, applications, identities, and certificates, along with their corresponding security assessments. Supported Asset Classes and Subclasses Unified Asset Inventory includes the following asset classes and key subclasses: Compute Virtual Machines Hosts Other Compute Container Container Instances (Docker containers, Kubernetes pods, ECS tasks) Container Images (registry images) Storage Disk volumes, object storage (S3), databases, file shares, blob storage Software Generic Software (applications, runtimes) Component Software (libraries, packages, dependencies) Application Web Applications Code Repositories Resource Cloud resources such as load balancers, DNS records, API gateways Certificate Resources (SSL/TLS and code-signing certificates) Identity User Identities Group Identities Role Identities Network Routers, switches, firewalls, VPNs, network interfaces, security groups Asset Sources UAI maintains visibility into assets regardless of how they enter the platform: Company-discovered and managed assets from native modules. Externally discovered or managed assets integrated via connectors, CMDBs, APIs, or third-party platforms. For example, cloud assets may appear both as: Assets managed and visible in Total Cloud Assets discovered and managed via Wiz and surfaced through company/Wiz connectors Asset Relationships and Context UAI is designed to support asset relationship modeling, enabling you to understand how different asset types are connected. These relationships help with dependency mapping, blast-radius analysis, and risk prioritization. Example Relationship Chain Web Application > Load Balancer > Virtual Machine > Container Instance > Software Component > Object Storage > IAM Role > User/Group You can view all your assets by navigating to the Inventory tab. Support for New Tokens for UAI For improved usability and searchability, we have introduced support for new tokens. These new tokens are available in the User Interface and documented in the token help. For more information, refer to the Token help from the ETM Online Help. Introduced Vulnerability Scoring System (QVSS) QVSS brings the power of Qualys threat intelligence (TruLens and RTIs) together with a CVSS-like 0–10 scale. It preserves QDS logic while expressing severity in a way that's easier to interpret and compare across teams. Why QVSS? QVSS addresses two common issues users encountered: Different scoring scales (CVSS 0–10 vs QDS 1–100) made comparison and communication difficult. Customers wanted the intuitive CVSS-style scale but without losing Qualys' threat intelligence advantages. QVSS Applies to QVSS is applied to every finding ETM manages, including: Vulnerabilities (CVEs) from VMDR and third-party scanners. Misconfigurations and policy violations from Policy Compliance, SCA, CSAM, and third-party tools. QVSS (finding-level) = QVSS Base ± Environmental Adjustments QVSS Base - It is a global inherent risk of an exposure. It utilizes real-world threat signals, including known exploits, CISA KEV data, Malware and ransomware activity, Threat actor TTPs, trending activity, and EPSS. Environmental Adjustments - It adjusts the score for your environment using risk factors, business context, and compensatory controls. This is the value displayed in ETM and used for prioritization, SLAs, and reporting purposes. Relationship with QDS QVSS is backward compatible, preserving the QDS logic and expressing it on a 0–10 scale (QDS ÷ 10). Existing QDS-driven automations and APIs continue to work. QVSS = QDS ÷ 10 (normalized) + CVSS-like severity mapping. QDS continues to be supported for existing automation. Currently, the QVSS score is displayed on the Vulnerabilities page, while QDS details will continue to be shown on the Misconfiguration Details page. You can view the QVSS score column on the Findings listing page. You can view the QVSS score in the findings details, as shown in the following screenshot. Introduced TruLens Contextual Threat Intelligence and Industry Risk Visibility TruLens is a new capability in Enterprise TruRisk Management (ETM) that delivers context-aware threat intelligence, industry benchmarking, and risk contextualization in a single, interactive experience. TruLens helps you move beyond static vulnerability data by aligning global threat intelligence with your organization's environment and industry profile. TruLens introduces a dedicated view within ETM that brings together real-world threat activity, industry-specific insights, and peer comparisons, enabling faster and more confident risk decisions. TruLens helps you make informed security decisions by focusing on the following areas: Threat Intelligence: Stay updated with trending and actively exploited vulnerabilities to safeguard your organization against potential threats. Industry Benchmarking: Analyze the threat actors specifically impacting your industry, allowing for a more tailored security strategy. Risk Contextualization: Evaluate your organization's security performance in relation to peers, providing a clear picture of your standing in the cybersecurity landscape. Prerequisite TruLens is available as a separate tab within the ETM user interface. To access it, connect with your TAM or Qualys support. TruLens Capabilities TruLens provides the following capabilities: With this new feature, you have a TruLens tab that includes the TruLens Home page and CVEs. TruLens Home Page – Unified Threat Snapshot The TruLens landing page provides an industry-tailored snapshot of the threat landscape, organized into intuitive sections designed to answer critical security questions. Key sections include: Industry-Related Adversaries Important to You Highlights the threat actors, exposures, and impacted assets most relevant to your industry and environment. Trending Threats Surfaces are actively exploited, and high-urgency vulnerabilities are observed across the global threat landscape. Peer & Performance Benchmarking Compares your organization's security posture and remediation performance against industry peers and top performers. Top Threat Categories in Your Industry Identifies the dominant threat themes driving risk for organizations within your industry. CVE Intelligence and Prioritization TruLens introduces an enhanced CVE view that categorizes vulnerabilities by urgency and real-world relevance. Newly Added: Recently published CVEs Trending: CVEs gaining rapid traction across the threat landscape Actively Exploited: CVEs currently exploited in the wild Manage Lifecycle State of Important Vulnerabilities Ingested via CSV With this release, we have introduced a new feature that allows you to manage the lifecycle state manually (Open/Closed) of urgent vulnerabilities and misconfigurations ingested through Generic CSV imports. This update brings speed, control, and governance to how you act on high-priority findings. These capabilities address the need to act on high-priority vulnerabilities while ensuring accuracy, accountability, and compliance. Key Features The following are important key features: Faster remediation lifecycle No dependency on connector sync for closure Better operational efficiency Reduces CSV upload overhead Improved accuracy Findings are marked closed immediately at the user's intention Flexible bulk actions Close dozens or hundreds of issues with a few clicks Direct Lifecycle Management from the User Interface This feature enables you to directly close vulnerabilities or misconfigurations from the User Interface, eliminating the need to upload updated CSV files or coordinate lifecycle changes through connectors. This speeds up and improves the remediation lifecycle for findings sourced from Generic CSV imports. Earlier, closing a vulnerability required updating data through connectors or uploading a CSV file. With this enhancement, you can select findings on the User Interface and request closure instantly. Availability This feature is available on the following tabs: All Findings Vulnerabilities Misconfigurations To access this feature, navigate to Risk Management tab > Findings tab > Vulnerabilities tab. Select one of the Findings from the listing page. From the Quick Actions menu, select Close Vulnerability. Enhanced Internet-Facing Tag Behavior and Risk Impact This enhancement clarifies how Internet-Facing tags are applied and used in Enterprise TruRisk Management (ETM). With this release, risk scoring and prioritization are now supported on Internet-Facing child tags. With this support, you have Clear visibility into Internet-facing exposure Assets exposed to the internet are automatically identified and consistently tagged, removing guesswork and manual effort. More accurate risk prioritization Internet-facing assets receive an automatic TruRisk uplift, ensuring high-exposure assets are prioritized correctly for remediation. Flexible organization without risk distortion Child tags let you group internet-facing assets by department, business unit, or function—without altering the system-defined risk logic. Let us see what those tags are: System-Generated Internet-facing Tag is The Internet-Facing tag is automatically generated by the system when an asset is detected as exposed to the Internet. Tag application is driven by system detection logic. User-Defined Child Tags Users can create custom child tags under the system-generated Internet-Facing parent tag. Child tags can be used to organize assets by: Department, Business unit, Functional, or operational grouping Child tags provide classification flexibility without altering the system-defined Internet-Facing properties. With this release, we have started supporting risk scoring and prioritization impact on child tags as well. Assets identified as Internet-Facing receive an increased TruRisk impact. The presence of the Internet-Facing parent tag results in an automatic risk score uplift (for example, a 1.2 multiplier). Enhanced User Interface for ETM With this release, we have introduced an improved user experience across all ETM pages. You can see updates across fonts, colors, typography, and buttons, making the interface more intuitive and easier to use. This release features User Interface and design system enhancements that improve visual consistency, readability, and usability across the application, resulting in a cleaner, more intuitive user experience. In this release, we have enhanced the user experience on all ETM pages. You can notice updates to fonts, colors, typography, and buttons that make the interface more intuitive and user-friendly. This release features improvements to the User Interface and design system that enhance visual consistency, readability, and usability throughout the application, resulting in a cleaner and more intuitive User experience. Key benefits are: Cleaner, more consistent screens Easier-to-read and understandable text Important information stands out better, with less clutter Faster comprehension of risk, status, and numbers User Interface Consistency and Clarity Introducing the new and improved User Interface with the following key upgrades: Easier-to-read labels and text, with ALL CAPS replaced by sentence-style text Consistent text style for status and source names across the application Uniform text colors in tables, filters, page numbers, and tabs Aligned colors and text styles for tabs and page navigation throughout the application Clear visual indicators for buttons and options, showing active, inactive, or secondary states Better emphasis on important information, with subtle styling for less critical details to help users focus Charts, Metrics, and Data Presentation You can view the following updates: Updated chart color schemes for improved clarity and accessibility Compact, more readable numeric formats for better visibility Refined color gradients for risk scores and meters to enhance interpretation Introduced New Color Code for QDS, QVS, and TruRisk™ Score In this release, we have introduced a new color scheme to indicate and highlight the criticality of the TruRisk™ Score, Qualys Detection Score (QDS), Qualys Vulnerability Score (QVS), and Asset Criticality Score (ACS). Previously, a different color scheme was used to indicate different ranges of values based to various levels of criticality. Now, we have added new color shades from a similar color theme to indicate score ranges and corresponding criticality levels. Darker shades represent higher scores that require more attention, helping you quickly identify and prioritize the most critical vulnerabilities. For example: The following is an example of the new color codes representing different levels of criticality and TruRisk™ Score: The following table displays Low and Medium TruRisk™ Scores, along with the corresponding color code changes. Revamped Findings Details and In-Product CVE Details Page This release enhances the Finding Details experience and introduces a new built-in CVE Details page within ETM, eliminating the need for third-party redirection and consolidating all CVE intelligence into a single, consistent workflow. With this enhancement, you can view: A dedicated CVE Details view embedded within ETM (no third-party redirection). Clear navigation between CVE details, detection details, and impacted assets. Improved context for vulnerability analysis by keeping all relevant information in one place. Consistent layout and navigation for easier review of CVE-related findings. You can now seamlessly navigate between CVE details, detection insights, impacted assets, and remediation guidance, with a unified layout designed for faster review and decision-making. The following screenshot displays the Finding Details page. The enhanced view combines CVE overview, threat status, Real Threat Indicators (RTIs), CVSS v3 and QVSS scoring, asset and business impact, and confirmed detection context (including MITRE ATT&CK techniques). Asset-level details highlight criticality using ACS and TruRisk, while the Impact Summary visualizes exposure across total and internet-facing assets, as well as affected business entities. A comprehensive vulnerability lifecycle timeline tracks exploit PoCs, patch availability, weaponization, and recent exploit activity, helping teams understand threat evolution. Clear remediation guidance and actionable indicators enable a faster response, delivering a comprehensive, threat-informed view of vulnerabilities within a single, integrated ETM experience. The following screenshot displays CVE details. Enhanced Identification Rules for Expanded Asset Class and Subclass With this release, Identification Rules have been enhanced to support the expanded set of asset classes and subclasses introduced as part of recent User Interface updates. Previously, Risk Management findings were based on a limited set of identification rules covering only a few asset types, such as vulnerabilities, misconfigurations, and web applications. As asset coverage has expanded, Identification Rules have been updated to ensure accurate and consistent classification across a much broader range of asset types. New Identification rules apply to UAI-enabled accounts. Reusing common identification logic improves: Consistency in findings classification Scalability as new asset subclasses are introduced Reliability of Risk Management results Key benefits are: Consistent and predictable Risk Management findings across all supported asset classes. Expanded asset coverage does not increase the complexity of interpreting findings for users. A scalable foundation for future asset growth and improved risk analysis. New updates include: Added support for identification rules across multiple new asset classes and subclasses. Standardized identification logic using a template-based approach, where common rule logic is reused across related asset types. Ensured consistent identification behavior for similar assets, such as: Container images, container instances, and container resources Vulnerability and misconfiguration findings across related subclasses To know more details about the Identification rule, refer to ETM Online Help. Introduced Simplified Asset Class and Subclass Filtering for Risk Prioritization This release enhances the Asset Class and Subclass filter used in risk prioritization by simplifying the user experience while maintaining existing risk calculation behavior. The updated design introduces a clearer, hierarchical selection model that makes it easier to filter and analyze assets across classes and subclasses. The following are the key areas of improvement: Simplified Filter Experience Introduced a new Asset Class filter component with a clear, multi-level structure. Asset selection is now organized as: Asset Class (parent level), Asset Subclass (child level, accessible via an expandable drawer) The new design reduces complexity and improves usability, especially when working with large asset sets. Improved Selection and Search Expanding an asset class displays all associated sub-classes in a drawer view. You can: Select an entire asset class to include all its subclasses automatically Select individual sub-classes for more granular filtering Added search functionality to the subclass drawer, enabling users to locate specific subclasses quickly. The search is scoped to the active filter component for better performance and clarity. Backend and Query Enhancements The filter continues to use the existing multi-group API, ensuring backward compatibility. Filter payload structure has been enhanced from an array-based format to an object-based format for improved clarity and scalability. Query behavior: Multiple subclasses under the same class are combined using OR Multiple classes are also combined using OR Risk queries remain consistent with previous behavior Saved Filters and Reusability Users can save filters as templates and reuse them across sessions. Saved filters retain Selected asset classes Selected asset subclasses The same filtering behavior is supported across: Risk Prioritization Risk Reduction workflows This enhancement does not impact existing risk calculation logic or projected risk score generation. Current scoring and prioritization results remain unchanged. Introduced Asset Grouping by Class and Subclass in Risk Management and Dashboard With this release, we have expanded the grouping and filtering capabilities on the Asset Listing page by adding support for Asset Class and Asset Subclass. The new Group By options help teams organize and analyze assets by type, like compute, containers, applications, identities, or software. This enables teams to quickly focus on the most critical asset categories during investigations and planning for fixes. The Asset Class and Subclass filters are available across: Risk Management (All, Vulnerabilities, Misconfigurations tabs) Prioritization (Vulnerabilities, Misconfigurations) Dashboard Widgets By grouping assets by class or subclass, users can better understand exposure patterns, identify high-risk asset categories, and prioritize remediation efforts. The Response tab does not currently support filtering or grouping by Asset Class or Asset Subclass. Improved Vulnerability Visibility with CVE Grouping With this release, the Vulnerabilities tab is enhanced to improve visibility and prioritization. By default, vulnerabilities are now displayed in a CVE-grouped view, providing an instant, consolidated view of all CVEs present in your environment. This CVE-centric view makes it easier to understand the scope and impact of each vulnerability by displaying impacted assets, exposure context, and risk indicators in a single location. Grouping by CVE helps security teams quickly identify the most critical issues, assess blast radius, prioritize remediation efforts, and track progress more effectively across the organization. Introduced New Tokens for Findings Tab Issues Addressed The following reported issues are fixed in this release. Component Description Unified Dashboard Fixed an issue where ETM widgets were not populating trend lines. Trending data is now correctly displayed when queries are applied. Unified Dashboard Fixed an issue where widget count sorting was not working correctly. Code changes have been implemented to ensure accurate and consistent sorting. Unified Dashboard Fixed an issue where a Manager User encountered a dashboard crash when accessing the External Web Apps – Build dashboard. The dashboard now loads correctly, including in incognito browser sessions. User Interface Fixed an issue where users encountered an Unauthorized access error when attempting to view asset details, even though the user account had the required roles, scopes, and permissions assigned. With this fix, authorization handling has been corrected, and users with valid permissions and scoped asset access can now view asset details successfully across all affected applications. Risk Management Fixed an issue where the ETM Purge Report UI encountered an OutOfMemoryError while downloading extensive purge reports. Memory handling has been improved, allowing purge reports to be successfully generated and downloaded as a ZIP file even for large datasets. © 2026 Qualys, Inc. All rights reserved. Privacy Policy. Last updated: May, 2026 Release Highlights Introduced Unified Asset Inventory (UAI) Introduced Vulnerability Scoring System (QVSS) Introduced TruLens Contextual Threat Intelligence and Industry Risk Visibility Manage Lifecycle State of Important Vulnerabilities Ingested via CSV Enhanced Internet-Facing Tag Behavior and Risk Impact Enhanced User Interface for ETM Introduced New Color Code for QDS, QVS, and TruRisk™ Score Revamped Findings Details and In-Product CVE Details Page Enhanced Identification Rules for Expanded Asset Class and Subclass Introduced Simplified Asset Class and Subclass Filtering for Risk Prioritization Introduced Asset Grouping by Class and Subclass in Risk Management and Dashboard Improved Vulnerability Visibility with CVE Grouping Introduced New Tokens for Findings Tab Issues Addressed Collapse Table
